    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer13196846658099442838">A haunting entry in the World Fantasy Award-winning Mythago Cycle<br/><br/>In <i>Mythago Wood</i>, Robert Holdstock gave us an intricate world spun from the stories of Irish and English mythology, a great forest steeped in mystery and legend, whose heart contains secrets that will change all who behold them.<br/><br/>Young Tallis is one such seeker. When she was just an infant, she lost her brother </span>
  <span id="freeText13196846658099442838" style="display:none">A haunting entry in the World Fantasy Award-winning Mythago Cycle<br /><br />In <i>Mythago Wood</i>, Robert Holdstock gave us an intricate world spun from the stories of Irish and English mythology, a great forest steeped in mystery and legend, whose heart contains secrets that will change all who behold them.<br /><br />Young Tallis is one such seeker. When she was just an infant, she lost her brother Harry to Ryhope Wood. Her adolescent fancies now cause her to suspect that he is still alive---and in grave danger. Tallis follows Harry into the primal Otherworld armed only with magic, masks, and clues left by her grandfather. Eventually the primitive forest gives way to Lavondyss itself, a fascinating and terrible realm where she is forced to confront the mythagos, physical manifestations of the legends of humanity's collective unconscious.<br /><br />Join Tallis on her quest into the ultimate unknown, and be invited into one of the finest and most compelling mythologies you will ever encounter.<br /><br />"A stunningly good book . . . conveys the haunting power of old heroes and lost gods."<br />--<i>Locus</i><br /><br />"Magical . . . It is rare to find a sequel which measures up to its original; but Lavondyss surpasses it."<br />--<i>Times Literary Supplement</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13196846658099442838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_40017759" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40017759" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40017759?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=award-nominee-british-sf">award-nominee-british-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=award-winner-britsh-sf">award-winner-britsh-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=favorite-ideas">favorite-ideas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=mythstories-and-legends">mythstories-and-legends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=1980-1989">1980-1989</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=6-star-books">6-star-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=love-those-words">love-those-words</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=award-nominee-locus">award-nominee-locus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=multiple-award-nominee">multiple-award-nominee</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40017759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12885346562543142512"><b><u>ANNOUNCEMENT TO FELLOW BOOK PROSPECTORS</u>: <i>Literary GOLD has been discovered....</i></b><br/>I am NOT hyperbolizing when I say that Robert Holdstock was a very special writer and his <i>Mythago Wood Cycle</i> is something unique and extraordinary in the world of adult fantasy, specifically mythical fiction. Like the “mythagos” of his stories, I consider Holdstock to be an archetypal figure representing the truly literate fantasy writers who steal our breath away and unleash our imagination with their eloquent, master</span>
  <span id="freeText12885346562543142512" style="display:none"><b><u>ANNOUNCEMENT TO FELLOW BOOK PROSPECTORS</u>: <i>Literary GOLD has been discovered....</i></b><br />I am NOT hyperbolizing when I say that Robert Holdstock was a very special writer and his <i>Mythago Wood Cycle</i> is something unique and extraordinary in the world of adult fantasy, specifically mythical fiction. Like the “mythagos” of his stories, I consider Holdstock to be an archetypal figure representing the truly literate fantasy writers who steal our breath away and unleash our imagination with their eloquent, masterly prose. Those writers that we love but at the same time make us feel like children because we know we can never even approach their level of skill. <br /><br /><i>Lavondyss</i> is Holdstock at the peak of the summit of the top of his considerable game. Before I continue, I do want to caution potential readers of the following: as beautiful as the prose is, this is not an easy read and will take a commitment on the part of the reader….but it is SO WORTH IT!. As soon as I finished this book, I knew that I was going to have to read it again because the central concepts, the story arc and details are so complex, nuanced and subtle that a lot can be (and in my case probably was) missed the first time around. Even if I absorbed only a tenth of what was there…what a singular reading experience!!. <br /><br />I am probably going to do a poor job describing the framework of the story but here goes. The concept behind the <u>Mythago Wood Cycle</u> is that there is an ancient forest in England that has existed since man first settled there. Mythago Wood (and several other places like it around the world) acts as a sort of “nexus” through which contact with different… call them "places" can occur (i.e. planes of existence, realms of the multiverse or what have you). These places are the physical manifestation of the collective unconscious of mankind (dust off your Carl Jung and you will have the idea). These places are accessible only by certain gifted and sensitive individuals and time and distance are very fluid so you never know what might be just up ahead. <br /><br />In these "places" dwell or are created "archetypal" figures (called Mythagos) that have been represented throughout history by characters of legend from mankind's collective myths. For example, heroes like King Arthur, Hercules, Odin, etc., are all shadows or aspects of the Mythago representing “the outsider who through his talents grew to become a hunter, then a warrior, then a king and brought new magic or radical change to a people.” Thus, the Mythagos reflect the underlying concepts from which our collective myths originate rather than the well known reflections of those myths that populate our most famous stories. <br /><br />I know…it is a bit confusing and I am probably doing a crap nasty job of it. <br /><br />As original and thought-provoking as the central concept is, what really makes the story shine is the superbaliciously, beautiful prose of Holdstock. He is maestro like and reminds me a lot of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/60177.Guy_Gavriel_Kay" title="Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Guy Gavriel Kay</a> at his best (another author who I really like). If you have never read Holdstock, I strongly recommend that you read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126192.Mythago_Wood__Mythago_Wood_1_" title="Mythago Wood (Mythago Wood 1) by Robert Holdstock" rel="nofollow noopener">Mythago Wood</a> and this book. <br /><br />As I mentioned above, these are not easy reads. This is especially true for this novel as it raises the bar considerably on the complexity and nuance that is only hinted at in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126192.Mythago_Wood__Mythago_Wood_1_" title="Mythago Wood (Mythago Wood 1) by Robert Holdstock" rel="nofollow noopener">Mythago Wood</a>. I intend to re-read both this and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126192.Mythago_Wood__Mythago_Wood_1_" title="Mythago Wood (Mythago Wood 1) by Robert Holdstock" rel="nofollow noopener">Mythago Wood</a> at some point as I think the enjoyment of the story may actually be better the second time around. For now, all I can say is that this was one of those truly special reading experiences that do not come along very often. 6.0 STARS. HIGHEST POSSIBLE RECOMMENDATION!!<br /><br />Winner: British Science Fiction Award <br />Nominee: Locus Award for Best Fantasy Novel<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12885346562543142512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_183940287" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/183940287" />
    <a title="Terry " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1262683-terry"><img alt="Terry " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316628758p2/1262683.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/183940287?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terry " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terry " href="/user/show/1262683-terry">Terry </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575043756" href="/book/show/1839628.Lavondyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=that-northern-thing">that-northern-thing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1262683-terry?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer183940287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13228328438845263330">2.5 - 3 stars (downgraded from a previous 5)<br/><br/>I am not quite sure where my previous 5-star rating for this book came from. I love Holdstock’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159161705' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mythago Wood</a>, but on this re-read I found the second book of the Mythago cycle to be a sl-o-o-o-w burn which unfortunately never really seemed to ignite. I may simply not have been in the mood for this book at this time, but I think it’s more than that. There are significant pacing issues that drag the book down and Holdstock’s desire to both go deeper into </span>
  <span id="freeText13228328438845263330" style="display:none">2.5 - 3 stars (downgraded from a previous 5)<br /><br />I am not quite sure where my previous 5-star rating for this book came from. I love Holdstock’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159161705" rel="nofollow noopener">Mythago Wood</a>, but on this re-read I found the second book of the Mythago cycle to be a sl-o-o-o-w burn which unfortunately never really seemed to ignite. I may simply not have been in the mood for this book at this time, but I think it’s more than that. There are significant pacing issues that drag the book down and Holdstock’s desire to both go deeper into the meaning of Ryhope Wood and also maintain an air of mystery and the unknown for as long as possible seem to work against each other.<br /><br />Instead of being a direct sequel to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8890494.Mythago_Wood__Mythago_Wood_1_" title="Mythago Wood (Mythago Wood 1) by Robert Holdstock" rel="nofollow noopener">Mythago Wood</a> we are told the story of Tallis Keeton, the half-sister of a secondary character from the first book (Harry Keeton), who seems to have been marked in some way by the magical wood that swallowed the Huxley family years before. Eventually we also come to meet Edward Wynne-Jones, the former collaborator of George Huxley who was also tangentially mentioned in the first volume, but no resolution to the stories of Christian and Stephen Huxley is to be found. This isn’t necessarily a problem, but I don’t think Tallis was a strong enough character to support the book (at least not the ponderous first half of it) and ultimately I think that the book Holdstock gives us ends up being all build-up with relatively little pay-off. There doesn’t even seem to be any real action until more than half-way through the book and even then calling it ‘action’ is a bit of a misnomer. Granted Holdstock is writing a more cerebral take on fantasy (literally so since the wood itself, the main magical element of the tales, is all about the manifestation of the myth-images from our collective unconscious), but much of what we are told is not new for any reader coming to the story from volume one and the story of Tallis’ childhood spent under the ever increasing sway of the wood starts to drag as Holdstock dwells on far too much detail with significant repetition. Alas Holdstock seemed to be trying to structure the story as though it was a mystery with a gradual (and I mean *very* gradual) build-up of tension and parsing out of knowledge. Unfortunately a lot of the mystery just wasn’t present for me (perhaps because I was a re-reader, but also I think because if you’ve read the first volume you *know* what the wood is about so a lot of the mysterious elements and slow build seemed highly unnecessary). In the end I think that half the time spent showing us Tallis’ childhood could have been cut/reduced without any reduction in the facts learned.<br /><br />In a nutshell we witness the birth and first years of Harry Keeton’s half-sister Tallis who grows up on a farm located near the mysterious Ryhope Wood under the shadow of Harry’s strange disappearance when she was only four years old. Having read the first book we know where it is that Harry went, but Tallis only slowly comes to understand this as she is apparently taken under the wing of tutelary mythagos and ‘trained’ by them to become something of an avatar for the forces of the wood (or at least for some of them, the eldritch presences spawned by the wood apparently always being in conflict). This then spins out into a tale of tragic love, divided allegiances, and the quest for the mythical Heart of the Wood, where all of the characters hope that their desires will be granted and questions answered. Of course it’s not as easy as that and the true nature of the wood’s heart, which mirrors the deepest and darkest levels of the human psyche, may be much more perilous than anyone imagines.<br /><br />One other thing that really bugged me, but that turned out to be more of a nit-pick in the long run, was the characterization of Tallis’ parents. It revolved around their utter powerlessness in the face of Tallis’ growing strangeness and apparent inability to do anything at all resembling a responsible parental action. This is an ever-present danger when using very young characters as your heroes, especially when you are not writing a YA novel. In a YA story I might be more willing to accept that the genre itself demands that the young child-hero Tallis have parents who are little more than feckless spectators whose useless obstructions are easily overcome. In a novel meant for adults, however, I would generally expect a somewhat more responsible, and conceivably effective (or at least proactive), response from her parents. Instead we see them apparently accept the changes and behaviour in her that, seen from the outside and with the lens of any ‘normal’ parent, would seem to be a descent into psychosis and mental illness without interfering in her ‘adventures’ and it rang particularly false. Given the fact that her father had already lost one child to Ryhope is it really likely that he will sit idly by while his daughter also seems to come under the sway of the wood with little more than a feeble complaints and disagreements? Granted that her father’s father was known to have a strange relationship with the wood and perhaps her family is thus more inclined to accept it as something other than mental illness, but I still had my suspension of disbelief strongly strained (perhaps due to the fact that I am now a parent) by the thought that any parent would let their child freely fall into the strange behaviours that the wood prompts in Tallis with little more than a concerned look and occasional ‘disagreements’. <br /><br />So a fair amount of disappointment for me on this re-read, though I will readily admit that Holdstock still manages to work with his mythic material in an effective and fascinating way. The resolution to the story is as ambiguous and circular as was the one from the first volume…I haven’t yet read further in the Mythago cycle (and must admit to being less inclined to now), but I hope that Holdstock managed to overcome his desire to keep spinning out the mystery and supplied some real answers, or at least resolutions, to the still hanging threads of the story of Ryhope Wood.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13228328438845263330" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2889342591624124691">If the very thought of wandering through a world of Carl Gustav Jung's archetypes generated from your imagination in a forest is enough for you to want to skip and click your heels together then this is the book for you. Hugely impressive the first time that I read it - the traditional fantasy quest is here transformed into a quest through the subconscious. A subconscious that teems with archetypes, in fact with an archaeology of archetypes, since as the child-heroine grows to adulthood within t</span>
  <span id="freeText2889342591624124691" style="display:none">If the very thought of wandering through a world of Carl Gustav Jung's archetypes generated from your imagination in a forest is enough for you to want to skip and click your heels together then this is the book for you. Hugely impressive the first time that I read it - the traditional fantasy quest is here transformed into a quest through the subconscious. A subconscious that teems with archetypes, in fact with an archaeology of archetypes, since as the child-heroine grows to adulthood within the collective mythological unconscious she journeys back in time towards more basic and fundamental archetypes in an attempt to find her long lost brother. <br /><br />Here there is much to enjoy about how myths shape our thinking and how by accepting or rejecting the patterns laid out in myths we accept their influence over the pattern of our lives.<br /><br />From the end of the novel looking back over your reading you can see that the author has worked with very familiar literary archetypes such as the quest, the Bildungsroman, the child stumbling into an alternate fantasy world and myths to create something distinctive and different. Although from another point of view this is the same old mineral water, just with a twist of lime.</span>

<div id="review_600338738" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/600338738" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/600338738?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586207604" href="/book/show/1839629.Lavondyss">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer600338738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11808007164689050894">Ryhope Wood in Hertfordshire, England is where myth comes alive. It draws images from the dreams and the collective unconscious of human beings and produces beings called <i>Mythagos</i>: heroes, shamans, fantastic beasts and beautiful damsels from the primordial depths of the psyche, walking about in flesh and blood. Robert Holdstock, award-winning author of <i>Mythago Wood</i>, follows up the first tale of his fantastic realm with an even more daring one: a journey to The Old Forbidden Place, <i>Lavondyss</i>, whe</span>
  <span id="freeText11808007164689050894" style="display:none">Ryhope Wood in Hertfordshire, England is where myth comes alive. It draws images from the dreams and the collective unconscious of human beings and produces beings called <i>Mythagos</i>: heroes, shamans, fantastic beasts and beautiful damsels from the primordial depths of the psyche, walking about in flesh and blood. Robert Holdstock, award-winning author of <i>Mythago Wood</i>, follows up the first tale of his fantastic realm with an even more daring one: a journey to The Old Forbidden Place, <i>Lavondyss</i>, where all myth is generated.<br /><br />Rivetting stuff, right?<br /><br /><i>Wrong.</i><br /><br /><i>Lavondyss</i> is one large snooze-fest. The story opens with a bang, then stays exactly where it is. The background is lovingly created, and we get to know more and more about the inner workings of Ryhope Wood and the location of Lavondyss: but Tallis Keeton's journey in search of her lost brother Harry (lost in <i>Mythago Wood</i>) just does not take off.<br /><br />As the story progresses, it begins to sound more and more like a notebook on world-building or a treatise: there is precious little action. And what there is, is disjointed. The story takes a huge leap between part one and two, and it is some time before the reader comes to grip with all that has happened in between. Holdstock's prose is good-one only hopes he had put it to better use.<br /><br />The two stars are for the world the author has created. I honestly cannot give any stars to the story - whatever there is! </span>
  <a data-text-id="11808007164689050894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13420696140931334725" style="display:none">This one is a slow burner, particularly in the mid part, but it is worth reading it because the end manages to tie up all the seemingly loose (and slow) ends.<br />The first 1/3 of the story made me adjust to the book because it is different from Mythago Wood. The story is set in the same universe, but the characters, structure of the novel, even narration - is different. <div id="review_62223587" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62223587" />
    <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper"><img alt="Kat  Hooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533675060p2/2177891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62223587?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat  Hooper" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper">Kat  Hooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2177891-kat-hooper?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62223587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18167720729106890224">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a>.<br/><br/>Many times I don't like sequels because there's nothing new to learn. Authors tend to give us all of their world-building in the first novel, so I'm often bored by a sequel. But Lavondyss blew my mind. It is, I have no doubt, one of the best fantasy novels ever written. <br/><br/>In Mythago Wood, Harry Keeton entered the forest with Steven and he's been there for years. We got the sense back then that Harry had some secret personal purpose for going in — it wasn't j</span>
  <span id="freeText18167720729106890224" style="display:none">ORIGINALLY POSTED AT <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>.<br /><br />Many times I don't like sequels because there's nothing new to learn. Authors tend to give us all of their world-building in the first novel, so I'm often bored by a sequel. But Lavondyss blew my mind. It is, I have no doubt, one of the best fantasy novels ever written. <br /><br />In Mythago Wood, Harry Keeton entered the forest with Steven and he's been there for years. We got the sense back then that Harry had some secret personal purpose for going in — it wasn't just to help Steven. His sister Tallis remembers him leaving when she was four years old. Her parents are distressed and assume he's dead. When Tallis hears what she believes is a communication from Harry and starts interacting with the wood, her parents think she's gone batty. But Tallis is determined to bring Harry home.<br /><br />Lavondyss may be the perfect fantasy novel. First of all, it's written in Robert Holdstock's beautiful style. I tend to be picky and demanding about style. A good story will not do it for me if the writing is pedestrian. It doesn't have to be poetic, but it needs to be interesting and creative — not just, as we say, "serviceable." Robert Holdstock's writing style, at least in these novels, is similar to Patricia McKillip's: straightforward, but kind of dreamy, too. To me, it's perfect.<br /><br />Secondly, Lavondyss made me think. It was complex and convoluted, and I didn't even know how complex it was until I got to the end. At that point I had to go back and re-read several passages so I could try to understand what had happened. It's not that it wasn't related effectively, but rather that Mr. Holdstock does not spoon-feed the reader. He does not divulge everything we want to know when we want to know it. We're given hints and impressions (and maybe even some false information from unreliable characters?) that must be accumulated and assembled. My brain had trouble bringing it all together in the end. What, exactly, is Lavondyss? Why do the mythagos travel there? What drove Harry into the forest? Who is he there? How is he related to the mythagos? How do Mr. Williams and Wynne-Jones fit in? Most importantly: what is the nature of myth, story, and legend, and where do they come from? (There are lots of other questions I could ask, but I'd be giving too much away.) Instead of leaving me frustrated, I am fascinated, and motivated to find the answers.<br /><br />Lastly, the story made me feel. The characters are endearing and I experienced their joy, pain, hope, and hopelessness. The ending was sad, happy, chilling, shocking, wonderful, and inconclusive. It stayed with me for days.<br /><br />I am still confused about a lot of stuff that I was hoping would be cleared up, but I'm happily confused. This is a story that requires a re-read in order to appreciate its richness. I've jotted down some notes — stuff I learned in the parts of Lavondyss that I re-read. I will have to go back to Mythago Wood and then read further in the series. I look forward to it and I can't wait to spend more time in, and learn more about, Rhyhope Wood.<br />See my <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/holdstockrobert.html" rel="nofollow noopener">review for Mythago Wood</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18167720729106890224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2782083539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2782083539" />
    <a title="Chris Berko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13125862-chris-berko"><img alt="Chris Berko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501820241p2/13125862.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2782083539?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Berko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Berko" href="/user/show/13125862-chris-berko">Chris Berko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2782083539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2983303234091126367">I enjoy sequels that remain true to the original story but do their own thing and don't try to be a formulaic replay of what came before. This one was different in structure, presentation, characters (although characters from book one are discussed and one makes a brief cameo), and story line but underneath it all you could tell it was part of the whole. There were moments of brilliance and long stretches of boring-ness and for a while I debated as to whether to give it or three or four stars bu</span>
  <span id="freeText2983303234091126367" style="display:none">I enjoy sequels that remain true to the original story but do their own thing and don't try to be a formulaic replay of what came before. This one was different in structure, presentation, characters (although characters from book one are discussed and one makes a brief cameo), and story line but underneath it all you could tell it was part of the whole. There were moments of brilliance and long stretches of boring-ness and for a while I debated as to whether to give it or three or four stars but the author stuck the landing so damned impressively I can now without a doubt give it four. I think I did enjoy the narrative and character interaction from book one more so than this but this one does go further in explaining how the wood works and the late Robert Holdstock gets credit for keeping the complex nature of everything understandable and entertaining, and this one does get complex. So while not as jaw-droppingly fun and original as book one, Lavondyss is still a notch above a lot of the stuff being written today. </span>

<div id="review_1639526510" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1639526510" />
    <a title="Simon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1071724-simon"><img alt="Simon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242398665p2/1071724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1639526510?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon" href="/user/show/1071724-simon">Simon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586207604" href="/book/show/1839629.Lavondyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1071724-simon?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1071724-simon?shelf=fantasy-masterworks">fantasy-masterworks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1639526510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10375185354010102680">It's nearly six years since I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/921215.Mythago_Wood' title='Mythago Wood by Robert Holdstock' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mythago Wood</a>, this book's predecessor, and I have to say that I went into this remembering very few details, only the general warm feeling of fondness I had for it at the time. I didn't realise afterwards that it had a sequel and it is only after this books inclusion into the revamped <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fantasy_Masterworks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Masterworks Series</a> that I spotted it and decided to pick it up. Now having finished this book I can't tell whether it's because my tastes have changed or whether it's just</span>
  <span id="freeText10375185354010102680" style="display:none">It's nearly six years since I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/921215.Mythago_Wood" title="Mythago Wood by Robert Holdstock" rel="nofollow noopener">Mythago Wood</a>, this book's predecessor, and I have to say that I went into this remembering very few details, only the general warm feeling of fondness I had for it at the time. I didn't realise afterwards that it had a sequel and it is only after this books inclusion into the revamped <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fantasy_Masterworks" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Masterworks Series</a> that I spotted it and decided to pick it up. Now having finished this book I can't tell whether it's because my tastes have changed or whether it's just not as good but I didn't like this nearly as much.<br /><br />We follow the life of Tallis (Sister to Harry from the first book) as she grows up and tries to discover the secrets of the Ryhope wood, the mythagos that are emerging from it and the stories they tell her. After seeing a vision of her brother Harry calling to her for help, she vows to find a way into the wood to rescue him. In another vision she manages to interfere with the "story of the wood" causing her future to change. The rest of the book is basically her pursuit of this aim and exploring the ramifications of the changes she made. At least that's how I interpreted it anyway.<br /><br />This time, the whole central conceit of the story didn't really make any sense to me and I found my attention wandering on more than one occasion for whole stretches of narrative. I struggled to feel motivated to pick up the book most of the time. Although there were parts that engaged me more, it was never sustained and the conclusion of the story was more of a confusing anti-climax and left me wondering what it was all about. I mean I just don't see what the point of it all was really.<br /><br />I see this series continues (loosely) on for several more volumes but I doubt I'll be adventuring back into the Mythago Wood again unless perhaps I decide to revisit the first book one day to rediscover what I liked about that and see if it still holds. As for anything else the author has written....I don't know.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10375185354010102680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_119704815" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119704815" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2276205-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285872916p2/2276205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/119704815?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/2276205-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780380711840" href="/book/show/1839630.Lavondyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2276205-kelly?shelf=ooh-pretty-wordses">ooh-pretty-wordses</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2276205-kelly?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119704815" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3454754331384745012">I have a bad habit of overusing the word “haunting.” Ergo, I worry that when I use it here, it won’t pack the punch it really should. Let me just say, then, that when I say <i>Lavondyss</i> is haunting, I mean it. This book settled into my bones like a hard winter. It will stay in my mind forever. I feel like I’ve lived a whole second life by reading it, and I’ll probably read it again at my earliest convenience just to see if I catch anything I missed the first time.<br/><br/> I had trouble getting into the pre</span>
  <span id="freeText3454754331384745012" style="display:none">I have a bad habit of overusing the word “haunting.” Ergo, I worry that when I use it here, it won’t pack the punch it really should. Let me just say, then, that when I say <i>Lavondyss</i> is haunting, I mean it. This book settled into my bones like a hard winter. It will stay in my mind forever. I feel like I’ve lived a whole second life by reading it, and I’ll probably read it again at my earliest convenience just to see if I catch anything I missed the first time.<br /><br /> I had trouble getting into the previous book, <i>Mythago Wood</i>, but I was glad I read it and am now even gladder, as it provides lots of background that helps make sense of <i>Lavondyss</i>. <i>Lavondyss</i> feels more like a “straight” fantasy novel, though; while there is still the idea that people create mythagos with their minds and that many of the book’s mythagos are personally tied to its central character, to me it feels that this time the story and the world stand more on their own and have more of a life outside of the character’s psychology. I feel less like I’m reading a slightly veiled book on Jung and Freud, and more like I’ve been sucked into a seductive, visceral fairy tale. I’m yet again reminded of a work of nonfiction — this time Robert Graves’ <i>The White Goddess</i> — but this time the analytical part of my mind was content to curl up by the fire and let Robert Holdstock spin his tale.<br /><br />In <i>Mythago Wood</i>, Steven Huxley’s traveling companion was Harry Keeton. <i>Lavondyss</i> centers on Harry’s younger sister, Tallis. Born when Harry was already a grown man, Tallis only knew her brother briefly, but she and her family are haunted by his disappearance. Tallis is an uncanny, precocious girl with an instinctive gift for magic, and it’s simply enchanting to follow along as she learns the ways of the wood and its spirits. Eventually, she journeys into the wood on a quest to find her missing brother. What happens after that, I won’t spoil, since I want you to be able to discover it for yourself. It’s an enthralling story, though, sometimes sad, sometimes beautiful, sometimes scary as hell. There are layers within layers, timelines looping around themselves in ways that don’t become evident until later, and an ambiguous ending.<br /><br />I love ambiguous endings, and I hate them. I love them and hate them because they stick with me, nagging at my brain, never letting me forget them. I lay awake for hours after finishing <i>Lavondyss</i>, prodding at the ending in my mind, wondering whether the “happier” interpretation of the ending might actually be a sadder one. I simultaneously wished Holdstock had clarified it and was very glad he hadn’t. It’s more memorable this way, and fitting for the MYTHAGO WOOD universe.<br /><br /><i>Lavondyss</i> has everything I love in a book: compelling characters, vivid prose, mythic elements, art-as-magic, complex character relationships, and just the right amount of ambiguity. It’s a fairy tale, the old kind with blood and revenge and jaw dropping wonder. It’s the kind of book that, when you finish, you feel the urge to flip right back to the first page and start over. (The only reason I didn’t was that it was the middle of the night. Blasted day job...) <br /><br /><a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/holdstockrobert.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Review originally published at Fantasy Literature's Robert Holdstock page.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3454754331384745012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1941745395" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1941745395" />
    <a title="Murray Ewing" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2454280-murray-ewing"><img alt="Murray Ewing" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495713950p2/2454280.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1941745395?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Murray Ewing" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Murray Ewing" href="/user/show/2454280-murray-ewing">Murray Ewing</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575043756" href="/book/show/1839628.Lavondyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454280-murray-ewing?shelf=magical-fantasy">magical-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454280-murray-ewing?shelf=fantasy-classics">fantasy-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454280-murray-ewing?shelf=literary-weird">literary-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454280-murray-ewing?shelf=re-reads">re-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454280-murray-ewing?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1941745395" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3738282684990084497">Less a sequel to <b>Mythago Wood</b> than a deepening of it, <b>Lavondyss</b> is the story of Tallis Keeton, younger half-sister to Harry Keeton, the scarred airman of that first novel. When, shortly after coming back from the Second World War, Harry disappears into nearby Ryhope Wood, Tallis is determined to one day follow and find him. To do so, she must learn the ways of this strange woodland, and begins her education in its dreamy, savage, folkloristic magic, endeavouring to learn the secret names and sto</span>
  <span id="freeText3738282684990084497" style="display:none">Less a sequel to <b>Mythago Wood</b> than a deepening of it, <b>Lavondyss</b> is the story of Tallis Keeton, younger half-sister to Harry Keeton, the scarred airman of that first novel. When, shortly after coming back from the Second World War, Harry disappears into nearby Ryhope Wood, Tallis is determined to one day follow and find him. To do so, she must learn the ways of this strange woodland, and begins her education in its dreamy, savage, folkloristic magic, endeavouring to learn the secret names and stories of the fields, glades, and trees around her home, while making masks in imitation of the three cloaked women who seem to be guiding her in her shamanistic initiation. But the moment when she must leave her parents, and her home, and venture into the woods on this long-planned quest, of course comes far too soon…<br /><br />When a book begins with a young girl’s burgeoning awareness of a magical world all around her, and her own growing ability to understand and even affect that world, you naturally assume this will be a coming-of-age story. But, after an abrupt transition halfway through the book, <b>Lavondyss</b> becomes what <b>Mythago Wood</b> (beneath its fantasy-adventure story exterior) is — a tale about loss. And, as <b>Lavondyss</b> does everything <b>Mythago Wood</b> did, only deeper, and weirder, and more harrowing, it’s both a far more difficult book, and a far richer book, if also being less easy to read.<br /><br />And I think that, up to now, I’ve been put off by that difficulty. Whenever I re-read <b>Mythago Wood</b>, I follow it with <b>Lavondyss</b>, intending to read the whole Ryhope Wood series, but I stall, simply because of how bleak, harsh, and wintry <b>Lavondyss</b> is — something exacerbated by the contrast between its beginning, so full of childhood magic, and its second half. But I think this time I finally managed to see through the bleakness to appreciate the power of this novel. It feels much less redemptive than <b>Mythago Wood</b>, but perhaps it’s closer to the sort of heart-wringing catharsis of tragedy, though it never hits the dramatic high points classic tragedy requires to bring its catharsis to a head. Instead, in its second half, it’s relentless, and unforgiving, and perhaps only truly cathartic in retrospect. (‘I feel violated, consumed; yet I feel loved,’ one of its characters says, and it’s easy to feel the ‘violated’ and ‘consumed’, and hard to feel that ‘loved’.) But it’s a book of undeniable, if troubling, power, and I’ll certainly be pushing on with the rest of the series this time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3738282684990084497" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_40618084" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40618084" />
    <a title="Vic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1821927-vic"><img alt="Vic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603217908p2/1821927.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40618084?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vic" href="/user/show/1821927-vic">Vic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40618084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4133938297299748149">We all make choices everyday that allow the immediate world to unfold in a particular way. This was the message in Holden’s book Lavondyss. We are creating and influencing life on multiple dimensions with every thought and action and these multiple dimensions and universes are all happening simultaneously. Trying to follow a linear trail back to where we or anything started is almost impossible. His writing incorporated concepts of reincarnation, transmigration of the soul, mystical or shamanist</span>
  <span id="freeText4133938297299748149" style="display:none">We all make choices everyday that allow the immediate world to unfold in a particular way. This was the message in Holden’s book Lavondyss. We are creating and influencing life on multiple dimensions with every thought and action and these multiple dimensions and universes are all happening simultaneously. Trying to follow a linear trail back to where we or anything started is almost impossible. His writing incorporated concepts of reincarnation, transmigration of the soul, mystical or shamanistic intervention and left me dizzy with possibilities. What I did come away with is that nothing is the way it seems. Everything appears to be connected in one way or one dimension or another. All the players seem to be related on some level. We have an unknown history with almost everyone we encounter and none of it follows a logical or linear path. Time and dimension are internal affairs and all is influenced by thought and attitude. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4133938297299748149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_342066903" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer6598850943377461406">Hard to know how to rate this one; the writing was excellent, and the concepts interesting, but reading it, I felt like I was slogging my way through a morass of unfocused melancholy. <div id="review_2973056274" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2973056274" />
    <a title="La petite Marianna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7313897-la-petite-marianna"><img alt="La petite Marianna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577840618p2/7313897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2973056274?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="La petite Marianna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="La petite Marianna" href="/user/show/7313897-la-petite-marianna">La petite Marianna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781473211995" href="/book/show/25742767-lavondyss">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7313897-la-petite-marianna?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2973056274" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14227121187098635149">Starting up my review (which truly is more of way to wrap up my thoughts around this read rather than a proper review) I must confess that I may have done some disservice to the book and myself in overhyping it… Since Mythago Wood (book one) ended up being such a favorite of mine, certain expectations were set and that usually doesn’t turn out so well…<br/><br/> That being said… what the fudge did I just read? Honestly, towards the end of the book it felt like a shaman/wise man from the Ice Age traveled t</span>
  <span id="freeText14227121187098635149" style="display:none">Starting up my review (which truly is more of way to wrap up my thoughts around this read rather than a proper review) I must confess that I may have done some disservice to the book and myself in overhyping it… Since Mythago Wood (book one) ended up being such a favorite of mine, certain expectations were set and that usually doesn’t turn out so well…<br /><br /> That being said… what the fudge did I just read? Honestly, towards the end of the book it felt like a shaman/wise man from the Ice Age traveled through time and space, found himself in my house, forcefully made me smoke, chew, inhale-you name it- something highly suspicious, then whacked me on the head and sent me into a really weird trip… Sooo, that may sound intriguing (and in a way true to the story…) but it was not what I signed up for…<br /><br /> Did I enjoy reading it? Ehhh, in a peculiar way yes, mainly because I still find the writing to be captivating and the themes very intriguing and to my personal taste. However, did I like the actual story? Ehhhh, not quite… which is a shame because the premise sounded so promising and I was so very excited to find out more about the deep lore of Mythago Wood and of Harry Keeton’s (a character from book one) fate. <br /><br /> The first part I liked a lot (although a bit slow) but as was the story progressing it gradually got too complicated, brimming with too many ideas and strangeness...(?) Now, I don’t mind weird, part of the first book’s charm was the otherworldly atmosphere and all the eerie stuff going on, but in Lavondyss it felt like it went completely overboard with all these concepts. <br />I do get it in part due to the nature of the journey the heroine undertakes but in the end the whole narrative felt more about being weird and inexplicable for the sake of being so rather than telling a story. It’s sort of like the actual story got lost in the way. <br /><br />There was a quest but many things and development that felt important happened ‘off-screen’… There were some things that were described to as if they were going to be a great asset to the heroine in said quest but never did play an essential role (which felt rather underwhelming)…. There were some backs and forths at times and certain things left unanswered…There was also lots and lots of exposition and explaining involved, which although interesting at parts was still too much. <br /><br /> I had a few other issues as well but ultimately what I feel Lavondyss lacked was probably the more streamlined/linear narration that made book one engaging. In Mythago Wood the story never really sidetracked despite all the mystical things happening -yes, there were some additional narratives thrown in for good measure but never lost its focus. It remained pretty straightforward and thus I got the chance as a reader to follow closely the journey the characters went through; while in Lavondyss it felt more like I was being described –amongst lots of other things- of a journey about to happen but never got to really experience it along with the character. I did like some twists but these too fell a bit short what with the main story being somewhat all over the place and many of the great revelations would have been more impactful if only the plot were more tight/coherent. <br /> <br /> So, yeah….(siiiigh), overall I was disappointed and it was a difficult book for me to rate (2.5 stars) because Holdstock’s themes resonate so much with me and I love Mythago Wood’s world, but this one unfortunately kinda missed the mark.<br />I’m not discouraged though, I will be continuing with the series at some point!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14227121187098635149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_893509257" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/893509257" />
    <a title="Phoebe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5176688-phoebe"><img alt="Phoebe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395949425p2/5176688.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/893509257?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phoebe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phoebe" href="/user/show/5176688-phoebe">Phoebe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone interested in exploring Jungian concepts who can be generous to reedy plots</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5176688-phoebe?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer893509257" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8081636351540502486">I would compare this story to really nice, slow cooked ribs. It's so good when you pick it up, but you can't help but notice that as you eat, a larger portion of it is falling off the bone. You are trying to eat fast enough, but it's all falling on the floor now and now your dog is horking it down without any appreciation for how long it took to make and ugh sauce is all over your hands and you're just holding the bone and that wasn't NEARLY enough food and it started out so yummy but dinner is </span>
  <span id="freeText8081636351540502486" style="display:none">I would compare this story to really nice, slow cooked ribs. It's so good when you pick it up, but you can't help but notice that as you eat, a larger portion of it is falling off the bone. You are trying to eat fast enough, but it's all falling on the floor now and now your dog is horking it down without any appreciation for how long it took to make and ugh sauce is all over your hands and you're just holding the bone and that wasn't NEARLY enough food and it started out so yummy but dinner is now ruined. RUINED, I say.<br /><br /><br />So to those who've read, my spoiler-y questions/thoughts:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />- During the whole image of eating at the King's Table, she assumes she's old, but also the sister. I assume that this is somewhat in reference to when she's the totem later in the book? That is the only stretch I could make, and it seemed flimsy<br />- What is with all the incest and proposed-incest in this book?! And no one seems to bat an eye at it.<br />- The love story with Scathach is basically all off-screen. I simply didn't understand why she would go wandering, searching desperately for this man when she seemed to be more genuinely in love with his friend(presumably first child was his as it survived longest). Emotional connection was utterly flat.<br />- So at the end, she isn't reunited with her father or with her brother at all, but with her memories of them. And neither of her "loves" in sight. That makes her "heaven" rather a bit more depressing, yeah? Not to mention the connection with her father/mother was pretty thin so...yay for spending eternity with that.<br />- She gets, what, 40+ years taken from her, and Harry is marching around in his 30s or so? I get it, time runs differently, but I was left wishing I learned more about what happened with Harry. Did he ever find that city from the fist book? Has his whole life basically been tragedy, too? Why is he also so unable to get out and/or find Steven, when the Huxley brothers both seem to have no issues leaving when they choose, as much as going inwards? <br />- Related: Why make Hollowings at all, why learn them, if all they do is get you stuck?<br />- Entire book boils down to a giant build up for a failed quest, but without the benefit of feeling as though the protagonist made a meaningful journey, as we see nothing BUT her journey's failure. Not one single character (even the "bad guys") really wins at anything they set out to do. Giant treadmill. Bleh.<br />- Just me, or were those masks and dolls largely ineffective like...80% of the time? I kept waiting for them to make a huge impact and they never really did beyond creating her initial screw-up with the birds.<br />- Why exactly did Broken Boy erupt into a giant portal that failed to lead them to where they needed to go? Thanks a lot, stupid deer.<br />- I was really sure that "I'll kill him!" speech Tallis gave to Broken Boy re: the hunter (Scathach) would come back to haunt her/them. Never did. Weird moment left hanging, or symbolism I missed? The cautious fawn is her, the naive one is Harry? I have no idea. She didn't live up to her declaration, anyway.<br />- I have only read this book and Mythago Wood, and I don't think I can continue with the series if they're going to end consistently without a clear resolution. I only have so much appreciation for that, and MW did a much better job at it than the second novel. I do wonder if the other books manage to have satisfying endings, though?<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8081636351540502486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3852792256144795513" style="display:none"><br />I read these books some years ago and have kept them with the intention of reading them again, but every time I pick them up I am *SPOOKED* all over again!!<br />Such was the profound effect they had on me...more so this book #2. <br />I am not usually such a scaredy pants and love a good gripping story but I have to confess that I was truly spooked out reading this book, while at the same time unable to put it down!<br />That is not to say that it is not a good book...on the contrary, it is so well written and the scenes so beautifully described, I felt like I was present in a wonderful forest....<i>BUT??</i><br /><br />If you are into Mythic Fantasy then this is hard to go past.     <a title="Tina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3704120-tina"><img alt="Tina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1354482535p2/3704120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101968095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14656350348913645248">I have just finished this book and I find myself kind of hollow, sad and full of after tought. I found this book to be more grim and it goes further than the first book in alot of things. It is so deep and I feel like I have just scraped the first layer, it is defently a re-read becouse I feel like I have missed alot of it. Not grasping the whole picture.<br/>It was a haunting; sometimes hard sometimes funny ride, to Lavondyss. But it was a good ride thanks to Holdstocks fantastic writing. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_708697547" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/708697547" />
    <a title="Sienna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39013-sienna"><img alt="Sienna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186090875p2/39013.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/708697547?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sienna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sienna" href="/user/show/39013-sienna">Sienna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39013-sienna?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39013-sienna?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer708697547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4842920539859400785">4.5 stars, really, but I'm rounding up on account of Robert Holdstock's very heady signature cocktail of Carl Jung and Joseph Campbell. Only it's not a cocktail at all, but a visionary paste made of viscous golden sap mashed up with wildwood and heartwood, berries and blossoms, vivid algae and foul fungal decay, marsh stems and leaves sharpened like arrows, a hallucinatory mixture to be daubed on the temples and third eye. Set aside your mortar and pestle and find a comfortable position as you j</span>
  <span id="freeText4842920539859400785" style="display:none">4.5 stars, really, but I'm rounding up on account of Robert Holdstock's very heady signature cocktail of Carl Jung and Joseph Campbell. Only it's not a cocktail at all, but a visionary paste made of viscous golden sap mashed up with wildwood and heartwood, berries and blossoms, vivid algae and foul fungal decay, marsh stems and leaves sharpened like arrows, a hallucinatory mixture to be daubed on the temples and third eye. Set aside your mortar and pestle and find a comfortable position as you join Holdstock in seeking Lavondyss.<br /><br />It's been a couple years since I visited <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/196106195" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Mythago Wood</i>
</a>, and that gap made my experience of the sequel (of sorts) more intense and pleasurable, less familiar, even as I welcomed a return to the push-me-pull-you atmosphere of Ryhope Wood, England's last primeval forest. Holdstock created a landscape that is at once ephemeral and timeless, real and imagined, terrible and enchanting, easily encircled by footpaths and impossible to cross.<br />Ryhope is impossibly vast with possibilities, and these are delineated only by the minds of those who interact with the place and its inhabitants, the strange, familiar creatures who once existed not in fact but in truth, because they are the heroes and villains of the stories we have told ourselves since the beginning to make sense of the life that has been thrust upon us. These mythagos live out their legends time and again; we glimpse them through the eyes of the seemingly ordinary English folk lucky or unlucky enough to live near the forest.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Do you think woods can be aware of people, and keep them at a distance?"</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />In <i>Lavondyss</i>, those eyes belong to Tallis Keeton, who knows that a promise made is a debt to be paid. (<i>"And a promise broke is a life choked."</i>) She gives her word carefully, deliberately, just as she interacts with the forest with which she has had a special connection — through family, through proximity, through love and loss — since she was very young.<br /><br />The rules that bind the cautious, obsessive youngster feel logical, dangerous: she must know a place's name before it will guarantee her safe return. She carves masks into different types of wood and names them, too, with shamanistic certainty. She glimpses a long-gone world bedeviled by winter and a young man at its mercy. She falls in love. She prophesies the results of her own actions. She renders her parents entirely useless, which would be a narrative flaw if this series of books weren't so reliant on atmosphere. This is a book in which a boy's skin is described as "a confusion of leaves," in which birds take on sinister significance with their "endless fluttering of wings, intense and urgent pecking of beaks," in which a castle made of stones that aren't stone is "as fine to look at as the fine lines on a mother's face." Who needs parents when you've got a forest?<br /><br /><blockquote>What is a mother's kiss?<br /><br /><i>The kiss of acceptance. The kiss of knowing. The kiss of grief. The kiss of love. There was no such thing as a mother's kiss. It was a kiss for all things. A son's kiss, too. It signalled the </i>rightness<i> of a deed. It signalled acceptance. It signalled love that goes beyond the love of a kiss. Yes. She knew it now.</i></blockquote><br /><br />Several reviewers have complained about the slow pace, the relative absence of plot, the ensuing boredom. I felt none of this, only a deep-rooted desire to visit Ryhope Wood, somehow, and an equally unshakeable fear of what I'd find in my own head. Look: if the atmosphere doesn't do it for you within the first fifty pages, move on. Life is short. Better to find the book that makes your brain fizz and your heart race. Further to that advice, I don't know if this is the sort of book one re-reads. Other reviews with downgraded ratings suggest not. But to read it for the first time was harrowing and delightful.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I generally try to avoid spoilers in my reviews, but... well. A few points.<br /><br />- Tallis's very visceral transformation late in the book — evocative of Merlin's imprisonment in a tree, and yet so different — is one of the most beautiful and unsettling sequences I've ever read. In the best possible way, I feel as though I aged centuries in the reading.<br /><br />- ...at the same time, I was struck by her behavior during her time in Lavondyss. Was Holdstock suggesting that Tallis in some way became a mythago herself, compelled to fulfill her own legend? <i>"What mind had created </i>this<i> mythago, she wondered in astonishment."</i><br /><br />- I had so looked forward to another appearance from Mr. Williams, the Cecil Sharp-esque collector of songs, and was disappointed when he failed to return. Fingers crossed he shows up in one of the later additions to the series. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />In summary: Magic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4842920539859400785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of high-fantasy stories</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ollie by: </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10305577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12483632123905308729">Tallis is a girl unlike others: as a baby, a mysterious stag that haunts the countryside appears at her house and leaves her a gift (of sorts); later, phantasmagorical figures follow her as she explores Ryhope wood near her home - the same wood in which her brother Harry (one of the main characters in the first book of this series) disappeared many years before. This piece of high fantasy tells the story of Tallis' entry into the fantastical world beyond the woods, and her attempts to reach Lavo</span>
  <span id="freeText12483632123905308729" style="display:none">Tallis is a girl unlike others: as a baby, a mysterious stag that haunts the countryside appears at her house and leaves her a gift (of sorts); later, phantasmagorical figures follow her as she explores Ryhope wood near her home - the same wood in which her brother Harry (one of the main characters in the first book of this series) disappeared many years before. This piece of high fantasy tells the story of Tallis' entry into the fantastical world beyond the woods, and her attempts to reach Lavondyss, where she thinks she'll find her brother.<br /><br />This novel is filled with beautiful imagery, especially around the creatures and vegetation Holdstock describes. But the story plods at times, nearly making this reader give up. However, the use of Jungian archetypes (re-named here as mythagos) and psychedelic craziness the book descends into in its later half more than compensate those less exciting passages. Holdstock wants to create a new type of fantasy - which he doesn't quite succeed here - but for his attempt alone this is a worthwhile read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12483632123905308729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9177939262843230255" style="display:none"><br />I immediatley started to read this after reading Mythago Wood. <span id="freeTextContainer17165321889182947387">The style of writing is still excellent, but the plot was difficult to follow. Often, things happened with no explanation for why they happened, and that bothered me. In other words, I generally need some kind of rhyme or reason to link events, in this book it didn't happen. lt seems to be the same problem that happen's in Robert Jordan's books-characters will wind up fighting some weird duel in other realms, with no explanation of how they got there or where the powers suddenly came from. Howev</span>
  <span id="freeText17165321889182947387" style="display:none">The style of writing is still excellent, but the plot was difficult to follow. Often, things happened with no explanation for why they happened, and that bothered me. In other words, I generally need some kind of rhyme or reason to link events, in this book it didn't happen. lt seems to be the same problem that happen's in Robert Jordan's books-characters will wind up fighting some weird duel in other realms, with no explanation of how they got there or where the powers suddenly came from. However, although this book seemed to border on nonsensical at times, it was a very well written nonsensical.</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review964218807">
